						<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><span dir="auto">Hybrid electric vehicle</span></h1>
			<div id="bodyContent">
								<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
																<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-navigation">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
								<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" style="" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width: 52px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gnome_globe_current_event.svg" class="image"><img alt="Gnome globe current event.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Gnome_globe_current_event.svg/42px-Gnome_globe_current_event.svg.png" width="42" height="42" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Gnome_globe_current_event.svg/63px-Gnome_globe_current_event.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Gnome_globe_current_event.svg/84px-Gnome_globe_current_event.svg.png 2x" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text" style=""><span class="mbox-text-span">Parts of this article (those related to Raw materials shortage) are <b>outdated</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(August 2013)</i></small> </span></td>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2nd-Toyota-Prius.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/2nd-Toyota-Prius.jpg/220px-2nd-Toyota-Prius.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/2nd-Toyota-Prius.jpg/330px-2nd-Toyota-Prius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/2nd-Toyota-Prius.jpg/440px-2nd-Toyota-Prius.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2nd-Toyota-Prius.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf12/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a> is the world's best selling hybrid car, with cumulative global sales of over 3 million units through June 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Prius3mi_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prius3mi-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <b>hybrid electric vehicle</b> (HEV) is a type of <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">hybrid vehicle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicle</a> which combines a conventional <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> (ICE) <a href="/wiki/Ground_propulsion" title="Ground propulsion">propulsion</a> system with an <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electric</a> propulsion system. The presence of the electric powertrain is intended to achieve either better <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> than a <a href="/wiki/Conventional_vehicle" title="Conventional vehicle" class="mw-redirect">conventional vehicle</a> or better performance. There are a variety of HEV types, and the degree to which they function as EVs varies as well. The most common form of HEV is the hybrid electric car, although hybrid electric trucks (pickups and tractors) and buses also exist.</p>
<p>Modern HEVs make use of efficiency-improving technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_braking" title="Regenerative braking" class="mw-redirect">regenerative braking</a>, which converts the vehicle's kinetic energy into electric energy to charge the battery, rather than wasting it as heat energy as conventional brakes do. Some varieties of HEVs use their internal combustion engine to generate electricity by spinning an <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator" class="mw-redirect">electrical generator</a> (this combination is known as a <a href="/wiki/Motor-generator" title="Motor-generator" class="mw-redirect">motor-generator</a>), to either recharge their batteries or to directly power the electric drive motors. Many HEVs <a href="/wiki/Idle_reduction" title="Idle reduction">reduce idle emissions</a> by <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/shut_down" class="extiw" title="wikt:shut down">shutting down</a> the ICE at <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/idle" class="extiw" title="wikt:idle">idle</a> and restarting it when needed; this is known as a <a href="/wiki/Start-stop_system" title="Start-stop system">start-stop system</a>. A hybrid-electric produces less emissions from its ICE than a comparably-sized gasoline car, since an HEV's gasoline engine is usually smaller than a comparably-sized pure gasoline-burning vehicle (natural gas and propane fuels produce lower emissions) and if not used to directly drive the car, can be geared to run at maximum efficiency, further improving fuel economy.</p>
<p>In 1901 <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Porsche" title="Ferdinand Porsche">Ferdinand Porsche</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Lohner-Porsche_Mixte_Hybrid" title="Lohner-Porsche Mixte Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Lohner-Porsche Mixte Hybrid</a>, the first gasoline-electric hybrid automobile in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-HybridCarHist_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HybridCarHist-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> The hybrid-electric vehicle did not become widely available until the release of the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a> in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> in 1997, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight" title="Honda Insight">Honda Insight</a> in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT2001_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT2001-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> While initially perceived as unnecessary due to the low cost of gasoline, worldwide increases in the price of petroleum caused many <a href="/wiki/Automaker" title="Automaker" class="mw-redirect">automakers</a> to release hybrids in the late 2000s; they are now perceived as a core segment of the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_market" title="Automotive market" class="mw-redirect">automotive market</a> of the future.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>More than 6.3 million hybrid electric vehicles have been sold worldwide through March 2013, led by <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Motor_Company" title="Toyota Motor Company" class="mw-redirect">Toyota Motor Company</a> (TMC) with more than 5.1 million <a href="/wiki/Lexus" title="Lexus">Lexus</a> and Toyota hybrids sold as of 31&#160;March&#160;2013<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">2013-03-31</span>)</span><sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>,<sup id="cite_ref-TMC5miHEVs_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TMC5miHEVs-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> followed by <a href="/wiki/Honda_Motor_Co.,_Ltd." title="Honda Motor Co., Ltd." class="mw-redirect">Honda Motor Co., Ltd.</a> with cumulative global sales of more than 1 million hybrids by September 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-Honda1mi_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honda1mi-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Corporation" title="Ford Motor Corporation" class="mw-redirect">Ford Motor Corporation</a> with more than 200 thousand hybrids sold in the United States by June 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Ford200K_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ford200K-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Worldwide sales of hybrid vehicles produced by TMC reached 1 million units in May 2007; 2 million in August 2009; and passed the 5 million mark in March 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-TMC5miHEVs_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TMC5miHEVs-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-KBB1009_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KBB1009-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Toyota0909_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toyota0909-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> As of June 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, worldwide hybrid sales are led by the Toyota Prius liftback, with cumulative sales of over 3 million units, and available in almost 80 countries and regions.<sup id="cite_ref-Prius3mi_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prius3mi-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> The Prius <a href="/wiki/Nameplate" title="Nameplate">nameplate</a> has sold 3.67 million hybrids and plug-in hybrids through March 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-TMC5miHEVs_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TMC5miHEVs-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> The United States is the world's largest hybrid market with more than 2.5 million hybrid automobiles and SUVs sold through October 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-DoEHEV10_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DoEHEV10-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sales2011US_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sales2011US-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USales1012_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USales1012-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> followed by Japan with more than 2 million hybrids sold through October 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-TMCSales1012_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TMCSales1012-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> The Prius is the top selling hybrid car in the U.S. market, surpassing the 1 million milestone in April 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-US1million_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US1million-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Cumulative sales of the Prius in Japan reached the 1&#160;million mark in August 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-JP1million_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JP1million-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_powertrain">Types of powertrain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Types of powertrain">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Types of powertrain" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle_drivetrain" title="Hybrid vehicle drivetrain">Hybrid vehicle drivetrain</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_DCA_08_2009_7004.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_DCA_08_2009_7004.jpg/220px-Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_DCA_08_2009_7004.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_DCA_08_2009_7004.jpg/330px-Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_DCA_08_2009_7004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_DCA_08_2009_7004.jpg/440px-Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_DCA_08_2009_7004.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_DCA_08_2009_7004.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf12/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid#Hybrid" title="Toyota Highlander Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Toyota Highlander Hybrid</a> has a <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle_drivetrain#Power-split_or_series-parallel_hybrid" title="Hybrid vehicle drivetrain">series-parallel drivetrain</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg/220px-2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg" width="220" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg/330px-2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg/440px-2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf12/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Vue#Green_Line" title="Saturn Vue">Saturn Vue Green Line</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">mild hybrid</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg/220px-Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg/330px-Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg/440px-Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf12/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The 2005-06 <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Silverado_Hybrid" title="Chevrolet Silverado Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Chevrolet Silverado Hybrid</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">mild hybrid</a> using the electric motor mainly to power the accessories.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG/220px-BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG/330px-BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG/440px-BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf12/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/BMW_Concept_7_Series_ActiveHybrid" title="BMW Concept 7 Series ActiveHybrid">BMW Concept 7 Series ActiveHybrid</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">mild hybrid</a> with an electric motor designed to increase power and performance.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hybrid electric vehicles can be classified according to the way in which power is supplied to the drivetrain:</p>
<ul>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Parallel_hybrid" title="Parallel hybrid" class="mw-redirect">parallel hybrids</a>, the ICE and the <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a> are both connected to the mechanical <a href="/wiki/Transmission_(mechanics)" title="Transmission (mechanics)">transmission</a> and can simultaneously transmit power to drive the wheels, usually through a conventional transmission. Honda's Integrated Motor Assist (IMA) system as found in the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight" title="Honda Insight">Insight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honda_Civic_Hybrid" title="Honda Civic Hybrid">Civic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honda_Accord_Hybrid" title="Honda Accord Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Accord</a>, as well as the GM Belted Alternator/Starter (<a href="/wiki/BAS_Hybrid" title="BAS Hybrid">BAS Hybrid</a>) system found in the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Malibu" title="Chevrolet Malibu">Chevrolet Malibu</a> hybrids are examples of production parallel hybrids.<sup id="cite_ref-UCSHbrid_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCSHbrid-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Current, commercialized parallel hybrids use a single, small (&lt;20&#160;kW) electric motor and small battery pack as the electric motor is not designed to be the sole source of motive power from launch. Parallel hybrids are also capable of <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_braking" title="Regenerative braking" class="mw-redirect">regenerative braking</a> and the internal combustion engine can also act as a generator for supplemental recharging. Parallel hybrids are more efficient than comparable non-hybrid vehicles especially during urban stop-and-go conditions and at times during highway operation where the electric motor is permitted to contribute.<sup id="cite_ref-UCSHbrid_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCSHbrid-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle_drivetrain#Series_hybrid" title="Hybrid vehicle drivetrain">series hybrids</a>, only the electric motor drives the drivetrain, and the ICE works as a <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator" class="mw-redirect">generator</a> to power the electric motor or to recharge the batteries. The battery pack can be recharged through regenerative braking or by the ICE. Series hybrids usually have a smaller combustion engine but a larger battery pack as compared to parallel hybrids, which makes them more expensive than parallels. This configuration makes series hybrids more efficient in city driving.<sup id="cite_ref-UCSHbrid_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCSHbrid-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Series_hybrid" title="Series hybrid" class="mw-redirect">series</a> <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid</a>, although GM prefers to describe the Volt as an <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicle</a> equipped with a "range extending" gasoline powered ICE as a generator and therefore dubbed an "Extended Range Electric Vehicle"<sup id="cite_ref-VoltMedia_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VoltMedia-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> or EREV.<sup id="cite_ref-VoltMedia_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VoltMedia-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Volt_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Volt-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle_drivetrain#Power-split_or_series-parallel_hybrid" title="Hybrid vehicle drivetrain">Power-split hybrids</a> have the benefits of a combination of series and parallel characteristics. As a result, they are more efficient overall, because series hybrids tend to be more efficient at lower speeds and parallel tend to be more efficient at high speeds; however, the cost of power-split the hybrid is higher than a pure parallel.<sup id="cite_ref-UCSHbrid_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCSHbrid-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Examples of power-split (referred to by some as "series-parallel") hybrid powertrains include current models of <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lexus" title="Lexus">Lexus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan" class="mw-redirect">Nissan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UCSHbrid_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCSHbrid-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_by_degree_of_hybridization">Types by degree of hybridization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Types by degree of hybridization">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Types by degree of hybridization" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">Mild hybrid</a></div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle_drivetrain#Full_Hybrids" title="Hybrid vehicle drivetrain">Full hybrid</a>, sometimes also called a strong hybrid, is a vehicle that can run on just the engine, just the batteries, or a combination of both.<sup id="cite_ref-Boschert06_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boschert06-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a>'s hybrid system, Toyota's <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_Synergy_Drive" title="Hybrid Synergy Drive">Hybrid Synergy Drive</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>/<a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Two-Mode_Hybrid" title="Two-Mode Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Two-Mode Hybrid</a> technologies are full hybrid systems.<sup id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ford_Escape_Hybrid" title="Ford Escape Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Ford Escape Hybrid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ford_Fusion_Hybrid" title="Ford Fusion Hybrid">Ford Fusion Hybrid</a> are examples of full hybrids, as these cars can be moved forward on battery power alone. A large, high-capacity battery pack is needed for battery-only operation. These vehicles have a split power path allowing greater flexibility in the drivetrain by interconverting mechanical and electrical power, at some cost in complexity.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">Mild hybrid</a>, is a vehicle that cannot be driven solely on its electric motor, because the electric motor does not have enough power to propel the vehicle on its own.<sup id="cite_ref-Boschert06_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boschert06-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Mild hybrids only include some of the features found in hybrid technology, and usually achieve limited <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel consumption savings</a>, up to 15 percent in urban driving and 8 to 10 percent overall cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-Boschert06_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boschert06-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> A mild hybrid is essentially a conventional vehicle with oversize starter motor, allowing the engine to be turned off whenever the car is coasting, braking, or stopped, yet restart quickly and cleanly. The motor is often mounted between the engine and transmission, taking the place of the torque converter, and is used to supply additional propulsion energy when accelerating. Accessories can continue to run on electrical power while the gasoline engine is off, and as in other hybrid designs, the motor is used for regenerative braking to recapture energy. As compared to full hybrids, mild hybrids have smaller batteries and a smaller, weaker motor/generator, which allows manufacturers to reduce cost and weight.<sup id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a>'s early hybrids including the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight#First_generation_.282000.E2.80.932006.29" title="Honda Insight">first generation Insight</a> used this design,<sup id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> leveraging their reputation for design of small, efficient gasoline engines; their system is dubbed <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Motor_Assist" title="Integrated Motor Assist">Integrated Motor Assist</a> (IMA). Starting with the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Honda_Civic_Hybrid" title="Honda Civic Hybrid">Civic Hybrid</a>, the IMA system now can propel the vehicle solely on electric power during medium speed cruising. Another example is the 2005-2007 <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Silverado_Hybrid#Hybrid" title="Chevrolet Silverado Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Chevrolet Silverado Hybrid</a>, a full-size <a href="/wiki/Pickup_truck" title="Pickup truck">pickup truck</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Chevrolet was able to get a 10% improvement on the Silverado's fuel efficiency by shutting down and restarting the engine on demand and using regenerative braking. <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> has also used its mild <a href="/wiki/BAS_Hybrid" title="BAS Hybrid">BAS Hybrid</a> technology in other models such as the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Vue#Green_Line" title="Saturn Vue">Saturn Vue Green Line</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Aura_Hybrid#Aura_Green_Line" title="Saturn Aura Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Saturn Aura Greenline</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Malibu#Malibu_Hybrid" title="Chevrolet Malibu">Mailbu Hybrid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plug-in_hybrids_.28PHEVs.29">Plug-in hybrids (PHEVs)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Plug-in hybrids (PHEVs)">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Plug-in hybrids (PHEVs)" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">Plug-in hybrid</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet_Volt_WAS_2010_8852.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Chevrolet_Volt_WAS_2010_8852.JPG/220px-Chevrolet_Volt_WAS_2010_8852.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Chevrolet_Volt_WAS_2010_8852.JPG/330px-Chevrolet_Volt_WAS_2010_8852.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Chevrolet_Volt_WAS_2010_8852.JPG/440px-Chevrolet_Volt_WAS_2010_8852.JPG 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet_Volt_WAS_2010_8852.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf12/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid</a> able to run in <a href="/wiki/All-electric_mode" title="All-electric mode" class="mw-redirect">all-electric mode</a> up to 35 miles.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in hybrid electric vehicle" class="mw-redirect">plug-in hybrid electric vehicle</a> (PHEV), also known as a plug-in hybrid, is a hybrid electric vehicle with <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">rechargeable batteries</a> that can be restored to full charge by connecting a plug to an external <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electric powersource</a>. A PHEV shares the characteristics of both a conventional hybrid electric vehicle, having an <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a>; and of an <a href="/wiki/All-electric_vehicle" title="All-electric vehicle" class="mw-redirect">all-electric vehicle</a>, also having a <a href="/wiki/AC_power_plugs_and_sockets" title="AC power plugs and sockets">plug</a> to connect to the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a>. PHEVs have a much larger <a href="/wiki/All-electric_range" title="All-electric range">all-electric range</a> as compared to conventional gasoline-electric hybrids, and also eliminate the "<a href="/wiki/Range_anxiety" title="Range anxiety">range anxiety</a>" associated with all-electric vehicles, because the combustion engine works as a backup when the batteries are depleted.<sup id="cite_ref-Boschert06_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boschert06-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HotFlat_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HotFlat-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Chinese battery manufacturer and automaker <a href="/wiki/BYD_Auto" title="BYD Auto">BYD Auto</a> released the <a href="/wiki/BYD_F3DM" title="BYD F3DM">F3DM</a> PHEV-62 (PHEV-100&#160;km) <a href="/wiki/Hatchback" title="Hatchback">hatchback</a> to the Chinese fleet market on December 15, 2008, for 149,800 <a href="/wiki/Chinese_yuan" title="Chinese yuan">yuan</a> (US $22,000).<sup id="cite_ref-F3DM_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-F3DM-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bwf3dm_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwf3dm-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> launched the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a> series plug-in in December 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-FirstRetailDelivery_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FirstRetailDelivery-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sales2010_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sales2010-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> The Volt displaced the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a> as the most <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel-efficient car</a> sold in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters112410_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters112410-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GCCEPAVolt_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCCEPAVolt-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="rellink">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_plug-in_hybrids" title="History of plug-in hybrids">History of plug-in hybrids</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_days">Early days</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early days">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early days" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lohner_Porsche.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lohner_Porsche.jpg/220px-Lohner_Porsche.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lohner_Porsche.jpg/330px-Lohner_Porsche.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lohner_Porsche.jpg/440px-Lohner_Porsche.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lohner_Porsche.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf12/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Lohner-Porsche_Mixte_Hybrid" title="Lohner-Porsche Mixte Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Lohner-Porsche Mixte Hybrid</a> was the first gasoline-electric hybrid <a href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobile</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1900, while employed at Lohner Coach Factory, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Porsche" title="Ferdinand Porsche">Ferdinand Porsche</a> developed the <i><a href="/wiki/Lohner-Porsche_Mixte_Hybrid" title="Lohner-Porsche Mixte Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Mixte</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-HybridCarHist_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HybridCarHist-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> a 4WD series-hybrid version of "<i>System Lohner-Porsche</i>" electric carriage previously appeared in <a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1900)" title="Exposition Universelle (1900)">1900 Paris World Fair</a>. The Mixte included a pair of generators driven by 2.5-hp Daimler IC engines to extend operating range and it could travel nearly 65&#160;km on battery alone. It was presented in the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Auto_Show" title="Paris Auto Show" class="mw-redirect">Paris Auto Show</a> in 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-HybridCarHist_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HybridCarHist-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> The Mixte broke several Austrian speed records, and also won the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Exelberg_Rally&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Exelberg Rally (page does not exist)">Exelberg Rally</a> in 1901 with Porsche himself driving. The Mixte used a <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">gasoline engine</a> powering a <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator" class="mw-redirect">generator</a>, which in turn powered electric <a href="/wiki/Hub_motor" title="Hub motor" class="mw-redirect">hub motors</a>, with a small battery pack for reliability. It had a top speed of 50&#160;km/h and a power of 5.22&#160;kW during 20 minutes.</p>
<p>George Fischer sold hybrid buses to England in 1901; Knight Neftal produced a racing hybrid in 1902.<sup id="cite_ref-engines_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engines-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pieper-patent-fig1.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Pieper-patent-fig1.gif/220px-Pieper-patent-fig1.gif" width="220" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Pieper-patent-fig1.gif/330px-Pieper-patent-fig1.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Pieper-patent-fig1.gif/440px-Pieper-patent-fig1.gif 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pieper-patent-fig1.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf12/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Figure 1 of Henri Pieper's 1905 Hybrid Vehicle Patent Application.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1905, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Pieper" title="Henri Pieper" class="mw-redirect">Henri Pieper</a> of Germany/Belgium introduced a <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">hybrid vehicle</a> with an electric motor/generator, batteries, and a small gasoline engine. It used the electric motor to charge its batteries at cruise speed and used both motors to accelerate or climb a hill. The Pieper factory was taken over by <a href="/wiki/Imperia_(car)" title="Imperia (car)">Imperia</a>, after Pieper died.<sup id="cite_ref-Victor_Wouk_p70-74_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Victor_Wouk_p70-74-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> The 1915 <i>Dual Power</i>, made by the <a href="/wiki/Woods_Motor_Vehicle" title="Woods Motor Vehicle">Woods Motor Vehicle</a> electric car maker, had a four-cylinder <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">ICE</a> and an electric motor. Below 15&#160;mph (24&#160;km/h) the electric motor alone drove the vehicle, drawing power from a battery pack, and above this speed the "main" engine cut in to take the car up to its 35&#160;mph (56&#160;km/h) top speed. About 600 were made up to 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-Beaulieu_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beaulieu-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The Woods hybrid was a commercial failure, proving to be too slow for its price, and too difficult to service. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a>'s 1928 <a href="/wiki/Experimental_Motorized_Force" title="Experimental Motorized Force">Experimental Motorized Force</a> tested a gasoline-electric bus in a truck convoy.</p>
<p>In 1931 Erich Gaichen invented and drove from Altenburg to Berlin a 1/2 horsepower electric car containing features later incorporated into hybrid cars. Its maximum speed was 25 miles per hour (40&#160;km/h), but it was licensed by the Motor Transport Office, taxed by the German Revenue Department and patented by the German Reichs-Patent Amt. The car battery was <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_brake" title="Regenerative brake">re-charged by the motor</a> when the car went downhill. Additional power to charge the battery was provided by a cylinder of compressed air which was re-charged by small air pumps activated by vibrations of the chassis and the brakes and by igniting oxyhydrogen gas. An account of the car and his characterization as a "crank inventor" can be found in Arthur Koestler's autobiography, Arrow in the Blue, pages 269-271, which summarize a contemporaneous newspaper account written by Koestler. No production beyond the prototype was reported.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predecessors_of_current_technology">Predecessors of current technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Predecessors of current technology">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Predecessors of current technology" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A more recent working prototype of the HEV was built by <a href="/wiki/Victor_Wouk" title="Victor Wouk">Victor Wouk</a> (one of the scientists involved with the <a href="/wiki/Henney_Kilowatt" title="Henney Kilowatt">Henney Kilowatt</a>, the first transistor-based electric car). Wouk's work with HEVs in the 1960s and 1970s earned him the title as the "Godfather of the Hybrid".<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Wouk installed a prototype hybrid drivetrain (with a 16 <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt" title="Kilowatt" class="mw-redirect">kilowatts</a> (21&#160;<a href="/wiki/Horsepower" title="Horsepower">hp</a>) <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a>) into a 1972 <a href="/wiki/Buick_Skylark" title="Buick Skylark">Buick Skylark</a> provided by GM for the 1970 Federal Clean Car Incentive Program, but the program was stopped by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA) in 1976 while Eric Stork, the head of the EPA's vehicle emissions control program at the time, was accused of a <a href="/wiki/Prejudicial" title="Prejudicial" class="mw-redirect">prejudicial</a> <a href="/wiki/Coverup" title="Coverup" class="mw-redirect">coverup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_brake" title="Regenerative brake">regenerative braking system</a>, the core design concept of most production HEVs, was developed by <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineer" title="Electrical engineer" class="mw-redirect">electrical engineer</a> <a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Arthurs&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="David Arthurs (page does not exist)">David Arthurs</a> around 1978, using <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/off-the_shelf" class="extiw" title="wikt:off-the shelf">off-the shelf</a> components and an <a href="/wiki/Opel_GT" title="Opel GT">Opel GT</a>. However the <a href="/wiki/Voltage_controller" title="Voltage controller">voltage controller</a> to link the batteries, motor (a jet-engine starter motor), and DC <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator" class="mw-redirect">generator</a> was Arthurs'. The vehicle exhibited 75&#160;miles per US gallon (3.1&#160;L/100&#160;km; 90&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>) <a href="/wiki/Fuel_efficiency" title="Fuel efficiency">fuel efficiency</a>, and plans for it (as well as somewhat updated versions) are still available through the <a href="/wiki/Mother_Earth_News" title="Mother Earth News">Mother Earth News</a> web site. The Mother Earth News' own 1980 version claimed nearly 84&#160;miles per US gallon (2.8&#160;L/100&#160;km; 101&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>).</p>
<p>In 1989, <a href="/wiki/Audi_AG" title="Audi AG" class="mw-redirect">Audi</a> produced its first <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/iteration" class="extiw" title="wikt:iteration">iteration</a> of the Audi Duo (the <a href="/wiki/Audi_100#C3" title="Audi 100">Audi C3 100 Avant Duo</a>) experimental vehicle, a <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in parallel hybrid</a> based on the <a href="/wiki/Audi_100" title="Audi 100">Audi 100</a> Avant <a href="/wiki/Quattro_(four_wheel_drive_system)" title="Quattro (four wheel drive system)" class="mw-redirect">quattro</a>. This car had a 9.4 <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt" title="Kilowatt" class="mw-redirect">kilowatts</a> (12.8&#160;<a href="/wiki/Metric_horsepower" title="Metric horsepower" class="mw-redirect">PS</a>; 12.6&#160;<a href="/wiki/Horsepower#Brake_horsepower" title="Horsepower">bhp</a>) <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a> which drove the rear roadwheels. A <a href="/wiki/Trunk_(automobile)" title="Trunk (automobile)">trunk</a>-mounted <a href="/wiki/Nickel-cadmium_battery" title="Nickel-cadmium battery" class="mw-redirect">nickel-cadmium battery</a> supplied <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> to the motor that drove the rear wheels. The vehicle's front roadwheels were powered by a <a href="/wiki/List_of_discontinued_Volkswagen_Group_petrol_engines#NF" title="List of discontinued Volkswagen Group petrol engines">2.3&#160;litre</a> <a href="/wiki/Straight-five_engine" title="Straight-five engine">five-cylinder</a> <a href="/wiki/Petrol_engine" title="Petrol engine">petrol engine</a> with an output of 100 kilowatts (136&#160;PS; 134&#160;bhp). The intent was to produce a vehicle which could operate on the engine in the country, and electric mode in the city. Mode of operation could be selected by the driver. Just ten vehicles are believed to have been made; one <a href="/wiki/Drawback" title="Drawback">drawback</a> was that due to the extra weight of the electric drive, the vehicles were less efficient when running on their engines alone than standard Audi 100s with the same engine.</p>
<p>Two years later, Audi, unveiled the second duo generation, the <a href="/wiki/Audi_100_Duo" title="Audi 100 Duo" class="mw-redirect">Audi 100 Duo</a> - likewise based on the Audi 100 Avant quattro. Once again, this featured an electric motor, a 21.3 kilowatts (29.0&#160;PS; 28.6&#160;bhp) three-phase machine, driving the rear roadwheels. This time, however, the rear wheels were additionally powered via the <a href="/wiki/Torsen" title="Torsen">Torsen</a> centre <a href="/wiki/Differential_(mechanical_device)" title="Differential (mechanical device)">differential</a> from the main engine compartment, which housed a 2.0&#160;litre <a href="/wiki/Inline-four_engine" title="Inline-four engine">four-cylinder engine</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1992, <a href="/wiki/Volvo_ECC" title="Volvo ECC">Volvo ECC</a> was developed by <a href="/wiki/Volvo_Cars" title="Volvo Cars">Volvo</a>. The Volvo ECC was built on the <a href="/wiki/Volvo_850" title="Volvo 850">Volvo 850</a> platform. In contrast to most production hybrids, which use a gasoline piston engine to provide additional acceleration and to recharge the battery storage, the Volvo ECC used a gas turbine engine to drive the generator for recharging.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Bill_Clinton" title="Presidency of Bill Clinton">Clinton administration</a> initiated the <a href="/wiki/Partnership_for_a_New_Generation_of_Vehicles" title="Partnership for a New Generation of Vehicles">Partnership for a New Generation of Vehicles</a> (PNGV) program on 29 September 1993, that involved <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ford" title="Ford" class="mw-redirect">Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Council_for_Automotive_Research" title="United States Council for Automotive Research">USCAR</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">DoE</a>, and other various governmental agencies to engineer the next efficient and clean vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" title="United States National Research Council">United States National Research Council</a> (USNRC) cited <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry" title="Automotive industry">automakers</a>' moves to produce HEVs as evidence that technologies developed under PNGV were being rapidly adopted on <a href="/wiki/Production_line" title="Production line">production lines</a>, as called for under Goal 2. Based on information received from automakers, NRC reviewers questioned whether the "Big Three" would be able to move from the concept phase to cost effective, pre-production <a href="/wiki/Prototype" title="Prototype">prototype</a> vehicles by 2004, as set out in Goal 3.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> The program was replaced by the hydrogen-focused <a href="/wiki/FreedomCAR" title="FreedomCAR" class="mw-redirect">FreedomCAR</a> initiative by the <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> administration in 2001,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> an initiative to fund research too risky for the private sector to engage in, with the long-term goal of developing effectively carbon emission- and petroleum-free vehicles.</p>
<p>1998 saw the <a href="/wiki/Panoz_Esperante_GTR-1#Q9_HybridPanoz-Zytek" title="Panoz Esperante GTR-1">Esparante GTR-Q9</a> became the first Petrol-Electric Hybrid to race at Le Mans, although the car failed to qualify for the main event. The car managed to finished second in class at Petit Le Mans the same year.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_hybrids">Modern hybrids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Modern hybrids">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Modern hybrids" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_7887_VA_11_09_with_badging.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_7887_VA_11_09_with_badging.jpg/220px-Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_7887_VA_11_09_with_badging.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_7887_VA_11_09_with_badging.jpg/330px-Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_7887_VA_11_09_with_badging.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_7887_VA_11_09_with_badging.jpg/440px-Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_7887_VA_11_09_with_badging.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_7887_VA_11_09_with_badging.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf12/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The 2010 <a href="/wiki/Ford_Fusion_Hybrid" title="Ford Fusion Hybrid">Ford Fusion Hybrid</a> was launched in the U.S. in March 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-GreenCar0409_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenCar0409-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Automotive hybrid technology became widespread beginning in the late 1990s. The first <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass-produced</a> hybrid vehicle was the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a>, launched in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> in 1997, and followed by the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight" title="Honda Insight">Honda Insight</a>, launched in 1999 in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT2001_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT2001-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> The Prius was launched in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and the rest of the world in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-tmc_20080515_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tmc_20080515-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius#1997.E2.80.932001_.28model_NHW10.29" title="Toyota Prius">first generation Prius</a> <a href="/wiki/Sedan_(automobile)" title="Sedan (automobile)">sedan</a> has an estimated <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> of 52&#160;miles per US gallon (4.5&#160;L/100&#160;km; 62&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>) in the city and 45&#160;miles per US gallon (5.2&#160;L/100&#160;km; 54&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>) in highway driving. The two-door <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight#First_generation_.282000.E2.80.932006.29" title="Honda Insight">first generation Insight</a> was estimated at 61&#160;miles per US gallon (3.9&#160;L/100&#160;km; 73&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>) miles per gallon in city driving and 68&#160;miles per US gallon (3.5&#160;L/100&#160;km; 82&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>) on the highway.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT2001_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT2001-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Toyota Prius sold 300 units in 1997, 19,500 in 2000, and cumulative worldwide Prius sales reached the 1 million mark in April 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-tmc_20080515_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tmc_20080515-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> By early 2010, the Prius global cumulative sales were estimated at 1.6 million units.<sup id="cite_ref-USAToday_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAToday-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LATimes_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Toyota launched a second generation Prius in 2004 and a third in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-SFGate2009_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SFGate2009-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> The 2010 Prius has an estimated <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">U.S. Environmental Protection Agency</a> combined fuel economy cycle of 50&#160;miles per US gallon (4.7&#160;L/100&#160;km; 60&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>).<sup id="cite_ref-SFGate2009_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SFGate2009-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Audi_hybrid_vehicles#Audi_Duo_III.2C_aka_Audi_A4_Duo" title="Audi hybrid vehicles">Audi Duo III</a> was introduced in 1997, based on the <a href="/wiki/Audi_A4#B5" title="Audi A4">Audi B5 A4 Avant</a>, and was the only Duo to ever make it into series production.<sup id="cite_ref-HybridCarHist_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HybridCarHist-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> The Duo III used the 1.9&#160;<a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a> <a href="/wiki/Turbocharged_Direct_Injection" title="Turbocharged Direct Injection">Turbocharged Direct Injection</a> (TDI) <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engine</a>, which was coupled with an 21 <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt" title="Kilowatt" class="mw-redirect">kilowatts</a> (29&#160;<a href="/wiki/Metric_horsepower" title="Metric horsepower" class="mw-redirect">PS</a>; 28&#160;<a href="/wiki/Horsepower#Brake_horsepower" title="Horsepower">bhp</a>) electric motor. Unfortunately due to low demand for this hybrid because of its high price, only about sixty Audi Duos were produced. Until the release of the <a href="/wiki/Audi_Q7#Q7_hybrid_.282005.29" title="Audi Q7">Audi Q7 Hybrid</a> in 2008, the Duo was the only <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> hybrid ever put into production.<sup id="cite_ref-HybridCarHist_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HybridCarHist-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Honda_Civic_Hybrid" title="Honda Civic Hybrid">Honda Civic Hybrid</a> was introduced in February 2002 as a 2003 model, based on the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Civic_(seventh_generation)" title="Honda Civic (seventh generation)">seventh generation Civic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Civic03_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Civic03-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> The 2003 Civic Hybrid appears identical to the non-hybrid version, but delivers 50&#160;miles per US gallon (4.7&#160;L/100&#160;km; 60&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>), a 40 percent increase compared to a conventional Civic LX sedan.<sup id="cite_ref-Civic03_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Civic03-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> Along with the conventional Civic, it received styling update for 2004. The redesigned 2004 <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius#2004.E2.80.932009_.28model_NHW20.29" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius (second generation)</a> improved passenger room, cargo area, and power output, while increasing energy efficiency and reducing emissions. The Honda Insight first generation stopped being produced after 2006 and has a devoted base of owners. A second generation Insight was launched in 2010. In 2004, Honda also released a hybrid version of the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Accord" title="Honda Accord">Accord</a> but discontinued it in 2007 citing disappointing sales.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ford_Escape_Hybrid" title="Ford Escape Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Ford Escape Hybrid</a>, the first hybrid electric <a href="/wiki/Sport_utility_vehicle" title="Sport utility vehicle">sport utility vehicle</a> (SUV) was released in 2005. Toyota and Ford entered into a licensing agreement in March 2004 allowing Ford to use 20 patents<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> from Toyota related to hybrid technology, although Ford's engine was independently designed and built.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In exchange for the hybrid licenses, Ford licensed patents involving their European diesel engines to Toyota.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Toyota announced calendar year 2005 hybrid electric versions of the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid#Hybrid" title="Toyota Highlander Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Toyota Highlander Hybrid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lexus_RX_400h" title="Lexus RX 400h" class="mw-redirect">Lexus RX 400h</a> with 4WD-i, which uses a rear electric motor to power the rear wheels negating the need for a transfer case.</p>
<p>In 2006, General Motors Saturn Division began to market a <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">mild</a> <a href="/wiki/Parallel_hybrid" title="Parallel hybrid" class="mw-redirect">parallel hybrids</a> in the form of the 2007 <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Vue#Green_Line" title="Saturn Vue">Saturn Vue Green Line</a> which utilized GM's Belted Alternator/Starter (<a href="/wiki/BAS_Hybrid" title="BAS Hybrid">BAS Hybrid</a>) System combined with a 2.4 litre L4 engine and a FWD automatic transmission. The same hybrid powertrain was also used to power the 2008 <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Aura_Hybrid#Aura_Green_Line" title="Saturn Aura Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Saturn Aura Greenline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Malibu#Malibu_Hybrid" title="Chevrolet Malibu">Malibu Hybrid</a> models. As of December 2009, only the BAS equipped Malibu is still in (limited) production.</p>
<p>In 2007, <a href="/wiki/Lexus" title="Lexus">Lexus</a> released a hybrid electric version of their GS sport sedan, the <a href="/wiki/Lexus_GS450h#GS_450h" title="Lexus GS450h" class="mw-redirect">GS 450h</a>, with a power output of 335&#160;bhp.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> The 2007 <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Camry_Hybrid" title="Toyota Camry Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Camry Hybrid</a> became available in Summer 2006 in the United States and Canada. <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan" class="mw-redirect">Nissan</a> launched the <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Altima_Hybrid#Hybrid" title="Nissan Altima Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Altima Hybrid</a> with technology licensed by Toyota in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Commencing in the fall of 2007 <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> began to market their 2008 <a href="/wiki/Two-Mode_Hybrid" title="Two-Mode Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Two-Mode Hybrid</a> models of their <a href="/wiki/GMT900" title="GMT900">GMT900</a> based <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Tahoe_Hybrid#Hybrid_vehicle" title="Chevrolet Tahoe Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Chevrolet Tahoe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Tahoe" title="Chevrolet Tahoe">GMC Yukon</a> SUVs, closely followed by the 2009 <a href="/wiki/Cadillac_Escalade_Hybrid#Hybrid" title="Cadillac Escalade Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Cadillac Escalade Hybrid</a><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> version.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> For the 2009 model year, General Motors released the same technology in their half-ton pickup truck models, the 2009 <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Silverado_Hybrid" title="Chevrolet Silverado Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Chevrolet Silverado</a><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/GMC_Sierra" title="GMC Sierra" class="mw-redirect">GMC Sierra</a><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Two-Mode Hybrid models.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ford_Fusion_Hybrid" title="Ford Fusion Hybrid">Ford Fusion Hybrid</a> officially debuted at the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Los_Angeles_Auto_Show" title="Greater Los Angeles Auto Show" class="mw-redirect">Greater Los Angeles Auto Show</a> in November 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> and was launched to the U.S. market in March 2009, together with the second generation <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight" title="Honda Insight">Honda Insight</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mercury_Milan_Hybrid" title="Mercury Milan Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Mercury Milan Hybrid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GreenCar0409_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenCar0409-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
